- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group (e.g., benzoic acid derivatives) tend to result in a high yield. This is likely due to the ability of the aromatic ring to stabilize intermediates or transition states. Examples include 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid and 4-aminobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives, particularly those with additional functional groups like hydroxyl or indole, tend to result in a high yield. This may be due to the ability of these groups to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups, such as polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.714 is lower than the previous consistent validation accuracy of 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective as the previous iterations in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy, along with the SHAP analysis, suggests potential underfitting. The rules may not be capturing the complexity of the data adequately, leading to a decrease in performance.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 3 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 frequently has a high negative SHAP value, suggesting it might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements Interpretation:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy suggests that the current rules may not be improving the model's performance. This indicates a need for further refinement of the rules to better capture the data's characteristics.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 3 and Rule 1 are often associated with discrepancies between predicted and experimental values. This suggests that these rules may need to be refined to improve the model's accuracy.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 3**: Consider revisiting the conditions under which thiol groups are considered to enhance yield. It may be beneficial to incorporate additional context or constraints to prevent overestimation of loading.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1**: Re-evaluate the conditions for aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-donating groups. It may be necessary to adjust the rule to better capture cases where these modifiers result in high loading.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce additional features or rules that capture more nuanced interactions between modifiers and catalysts. This could help address potential underfitting by providing the model with more information to learn from.

4. **Iterative Refinement Using SHAP Insights:**
   - Use the insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules. For example, experiment with modifying Rule 3 and Rule 1 based on the specific cases where they lead to discrepancies, and evaluate the impact on model performance.

5. **Consider Additional Data or Features:**
   - If possible, incorporate additional data or features that could provide more context for the rules. This could include structural features of the molecules or additional experimental conditions that might influence loading.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve the model's accuracy and generalization, ultimately leading to better predictions of modifier loading on catalysts.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group with additional electron-donating groups such as amino or hydroxyl groups tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize intermediates or transition states through resonance and hydrogen bonding. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives, especially those with aromatic side chains or additional functional groups like indole, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (thiol) tend to result in a high yield. Thiol groups can interact strongly with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalyst stability or activity. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups, such as polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with both aromatic and aliphatic carboxylic acid groups, especially when combined with electron-donating groups, tend to result in a high yield. These combinations can enhance resonance stabilization and metal coordination. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 also frequently has a high negative SHAP value, suggesting it might be underestimating the loading in these cases.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group with additional electron-donating groups such as amino or hydroxyl groups tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize intermediates or transition states through resonance and hydrogen bonding. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives, especially those with aromatic side chains or additional functional groups like indole, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (thiol) tend to result in a high yield. Thiol groups can interact strongly with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalyst stability or activity. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups, such as polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with both aromatic and aliphatic carboxylic acid groups, especially when combined with electron-donating groups, tend to result in a high yield. These combinations can enhance resonance stabilization and metal coordination. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6571 is lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective as the previous iterations in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy compared to previous iterations suggests potential underfitting. The model may not be capturing the complexity of the data adequately, possibly due to overly generalized rules or insufficient feature representation.

3. **Discrepancy Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be contributing to overestimation.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 also frequently has a high negative SHAP value, suggesting it might be contributing to underestimation.

4. **Accuracy Improvement Interpretation:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement in the rules. The decrease in accuracy indicates that the current set of rules may not be capturing the necessary features or interactions effectively.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that Rule 1 has a significant impact on predictions, both positively and negatively. This suggests that Rule 1 might be too broad or not specific enough, leading to both overestimations and underestimations.
   - Rules with consistently low SHAP values, such as Rule 5 and Rule 6, might not be contributing significantly to the model's predictions and could be candidates for refinement or removal.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 1:**
   - Given its significant impact on predictions, Rule 1 should be refined to better capture the specific conditions under which aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-donating groups lead to high yield. Consider adding more specific conditions or exceptions to this rule to reduce overestimation and underestimation.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - Rules that consistently show high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings, such as Rule 4 and Rule 5, should be reviewed. These rules might need to be adjusted to better capture the conditions under which they predict low yield.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider expanding the feature set or introducing new rules that capture additional chemical interactions or structural features. This could help the model better capture the complexity of the data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that have a significant impact on predictions and explore ways to make them more specific or nuanced. This iterative process can help improve the model's accuracy and generalization over time.

5. **Evaluate Rule Effectiveness:**
   - Continuously evaluate the effectiveness of each rule by monitoring changes in SHAP values and accuracy metrics. This will help identify which rules are contributing positively to the model's performance and which need further refinement.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group with additional electron-donating groups such as amino or hydroxyl groups tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize intermediates or transition states through resonance and hydrogen bonding. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives, especially those with aromatic side chains or additional functional groups like indole, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (thiol) tend to result in a high yield. Thiol groups can interact strongly with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalyst stability or activity. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups, such as polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with both aromatic and aliphatic carboxylic acid groups, especially when combined with electron-donating groups, tend to result in a high yield. These combinations can enhance resonance stabilization and metal coordination. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with both aromatic and aliphatic carboxylic acid groups, especially when combined with electron-donating groups, tend to result in a high yield. These combinations can enhance resonance stabilization and metal coordination. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group with additional electron-donating groups such as amino or hydroxyl groups tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize intermediates or transition states through resonance and hydrogen bonding. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives, especially those with aromatic side chains or additional functional groups like indole, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (thiol) tend to result in a high yield. Thiol groups can interact strongly with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalyst stability or activity. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups, such as polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups can stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with both aromatic and aliphatic carboxylic acid groups, especially when combined with electron-donating groups, tend to result in a high yield. These combinations can enhance resonance stabilization and metal coordination. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Overestimated Loadings**: For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value. This suggests that Rule 1 might be too broad or not specific enough, leading to overestimation.
- **Underestimated Loadings**: For molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 4 often has a high negative SHAP value. This indicates that Rule 4 might be too restrictive or not capturing all relevant features, leading to underestimation.

#### 4. Interpret Accuracy Improvements:
The accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The fluctuations in accuracy indicate that the current rules are not yet optimal and that further refinement could lead to better performance.

#### 5. Derive Insights from SHAP Analysis:
The SHAP analysis highlights specific rules that contribute significantly to prediction errors. For instance, Rule 1's high positive SHAP values in overestimated cases suggest it might be too inclusive. Rule 4's high negative SHAP values in underestimated cases suggest it might be missing key features that contribute to high loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 1**: Given its high positive SHAP values in overestimated cases, Rule 1 should be refined to be more specific. Consider narrowing the scope of electron-donating groups or adding additional conditions to better capture the nuances of high loading.

2. **Adjust Rule 4**: Since Rule 4 has high negative SHAP values in underestimated cases, it should be expanded to include additional features or conditions that might contribute to high loading. This could involve considering other types of interactions or structural features that polyols might have.

3. **Improve Generalization**: To address potential underfitting, consider introducing new rules or modifying existing ones to capture more complex interactions. This could involve incorporating additional chemical features or considering the context of the entire molecule rather than isolated functional groups.

4. **Iterative Refinement**: Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with extreme SHAP values and adjust them to better align with experimental data. This iterative process should help in gradually improving the model's accuracy and generalization.

5. **Incorporate Additional Data**: If possible, incorporate more diverse data to help the model learn a wider range of interactions and improve its ability to generalize to new molecules.

By focusing on these areas, the rules can be refined to improve the model's predictive accuracy and robustness, ultimately leading to better alignment with experimental results.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group with ortho or para-positioned amino groups tend to result in a high yield. These positions allow for effective resonance stabilization and hydrogen bonding. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives that have aromatic side chains, such as phenyl or indole groups, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can coordinate with metal centers and enhance catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing both sulfanyl groups and aromatic rings tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction, while the aromatic ring provides resonance stabilization. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups in close proximity, such as vicinal diols or polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form strong hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can create a balance that stabilizes transition states. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

   - The current rules seem to be struggling with effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.5143. This is a significant drop from previous accuracies, which were consistently around 0.6571 to 0.7714. This suggests that the rules may not be capturing the underlying patterns in the data effectively.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy suggests potential overfitting to the training data. The model might be capturing noise rather than the true signal, leading to poor generalization on unseen data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 4 often has a high negative SHAP value, suggesting it might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The significant drop in accuracy indicates that the current rules are not optimal and need refinement to improve prediction performance.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that Rule 1 and Rule 4 are frequently involved in discrepancies between predicted and experimental values. This suggests that these rules may need to be refined to better capture the relationship between molecular features and catalyst loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1**: Consider revising the conditions under which aromatic carboxylic acids with amino groups are considered high yield. It may be beneficial to incorporate additional structural features or context that influence their effectiveness.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 4**: Re-evaluate the impact of multiple hydroxyl groups. It may be necessary to consider the spatial arrangement or the presence of other functional groups that could influence their interaction with the catalyst.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more diverse training data to help the model generalize better. This could involve including a wider variety of molecular structures or conditions to prevent overfitting.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. For example, if certain rules consistently contribute to incorrect predictions, consider modifying or combining them with other rules to improve accuracy.

5. **Incorporate Additional Features:**
   - Consider adding new features or rules that capture other relevant chemical properties, such as steric effects or electronic properties, which may influence catalyst loading.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve their predictive power and generalization, ultimately leading to better performance on both training and validation datasets.

These structures can coordinate with metal centers and enhance catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing both sulfanyl groups and aromatic rings tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction, while the aromatic ring provides resonance stabilization. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups in close proximity, such as vicinal diols or polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form strong hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can create a balance that stabilizes transition states. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can create a balance that stabilizes transition states. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring and a hydroxyl group in the para position tend to result in a high yield. The hydroxyl group can enhance metal coordination and stabilize intermediates. An example is 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

   - Rules with high negative SHAP values, such as Rule 5 and Rule 6, should be adjusted to better capture cases where the experimental loading is high. This could involve relaxing some constraints or adding additional conditions that account for overlooked interactions.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address overfitting, consider simplifying some rules or combining similar rules to reduce complexity. For underfitting, explore adding new rules that capture additional chemical interactions or structural features not currently considered.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that consistently show high SHAP values (positive or negative) and evaluate their impact on different datasets. This iterative process can help in achieving a balance between specificity and generalization.

5. **Incorporate Additional Features:**
   - Consider incorporating additional chemical features or interactions that might not be captured by the current rules. This could involve exploring new types of modifiers or structural motifs that have shown promise in other studies.

By following these recommendations, the rules can be refined to improve their predictive accuracy and robustness across different datasets. This iterative approach, guided by SHAP analysis, can help in achieving a more reliable and generalizable model.

**Rule 9**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield.

**Rule 10**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an aromatic carboxylic acid group with ortho or para-positioned amino groups tend to result in a high yield. These positions allow for effective resonance stabilization and hydrogen bonding. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with amino acids or derivatives that have aromatic side chains, such as phenyl or indole groups, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can coordinate with metal centers and enhance catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups in close proximity, such as vicinal diols or polyols, tend to result in a high yield. These groups can form strong hydrogen bonds and coordinate with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. An example is (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. These groups stabilize negative charges in transition states. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to a lack of specific interactions with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with ether linkages and no additional coordinating groups tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to limited interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine may destabilize intermediates or transition states. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with ketone groups on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The ketone group may not interact effectively with the catalyst. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with pyridine rings without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The pyridine nitrogen may not coordinate effectively with the catalyst in the absence of other groups. An example is 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with formyl groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may not provide sufficient stabilization or interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid and 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a combination of aromatic rings and sulfanyl groups tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic ring can provide resonance stabilization, while the sulfanyl group enhances metal interaction. An example is 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in a high yield. The pyridine nitrogen can coordinate with the metal center, while the carboxylic acid group can stabilize intermediates. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and additional carboxylic acid groups tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran can provide aromatic stabilization, while the carboxylic acid groups enhance metal coordination. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups on an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can create a balance that stabilizes transition states. An example is 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring and a hydroxyl group in the para position tend to result in a high yield. The hydroxyl group can enhance metal coordination and stabilize intermediates. An example is 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------
